5.7       Billing activities are carried out regularly and are predominantly
manual operations, although YWSC has started to experiment with computerized
billing and accounting. Bills for all residential users and some small
businesses are presented in person and charges collected by hand. Industrial
and most institutional consumers pay their bills through direct debit from
their bank accounts. Meter reading is separate from presentation of bills and
collection of charges.
5.8       Despite its labor-intensive nature, the billing and collection
system works well with little scope for abuse, few bad debts and rapid
collection of charges due. However, there is little doubt that the process
could be streamlined with the introduction of microcomputer-based billing
systems. This would eliminate much of the tedious work, reduce staffing and
enhance analytical possibilities. The proposed project includes a small
component (under institutional development) to finance additional data
processing equipment and software for this purpose.
Water Company Accounting
5.9       Each operating department of a water company maintains financial
accounts which are consolidated with those of all other operating departments
of the same company. The units prepare accounting information in a timely
fashion each month; headquarters compile consolidated income statements,
balance sheets and supplementary statements monthly, quarterly and annually.
Accounts are prepared on an historical cost and accrual basis using a double
entry system. Assets are not revalued and are depreciated on a straight line
basis.



- 27 -
5.10      Up to the present, virtually all major capital investments have
been financed by grants from the municipal, provincial or central governments.
The accounts of construction units are not consolidated with the water
companies' accounts. As a result, construction work-in-progress is not
properly reflected in the books of the water companies. For the future,
however, major new assets will be partially financed with loans made to the
water companies who will be responsible for their repayments. Accordingly tile
water companies' future accounts will more properly reflect the full value of
new assets under construction.
Water ComRany Management
5.11      Metering:  Industrial and institutional users have essentially been
fully metered in all three cities by the end of 1990. The metering coverages
of domestic consumers are 66 percent in Shenyang, 49 percent in Fuxin and 72
percent in Yingkou. The water supply companies will make efforts to improve
domestic consumer metering. Agreed targets for metering coverage are shown in
Annex 18, and will be included in an action plan (para. 5.15) to be carried
out by the water supply companies.
5.12      Meter Maintenance:  Every city has a Bureau of Measures which
requires water meters to be replaced for maintenance every 3 to 5 years. In
addition meters are removed for checking whenever readings deviate
substantially from previous recorded readings. This practice is normal and is
acceptable. Sufficient capability exists in each company for maintenance and
repair of water meters.
5.13      Leakage Management:  In all three cities, leaks are repaired when
they become visible at the ground surface or begin to affect water pressure.
This is reflected in the size of the maintenance crews for leak detection and
pipe repair which number 340 for Shenyang, 85 for Yingkou and 250 for Fuxin;
only about 5 percent are engaged in leak detection. No effort has been made
to detect invisible leaks, which contribute a substantial amount of water loss
before they become visible or noticeable. Because of the efficiency in
collecting water charges and the strong meter maintenance program, it is
likely that most of the unaccounted for water would arise from system leakage.
The leakage situation will be aggravated as the water supply is improved and
water pressures are raised. During negotiations an action plan (para. 5.15)
was discussed in which the Liaoning Government would undertake to cause the
water supply companies to: (a) carry out an immediate survey of the amount of
leakage in their systems; and (b) carry out a systematic leak detection
program with the aim of reducing leakage and maintaining it at acceptable
levels. The procurement of suitable leak detection equipment has been
included in the project, and the technical advisors to be assigned to the
water supply companies will provide the necessary technical assistance.
5.14      Staffing Level:  The indicators of staff performance of the three
water supply companies, as measured by the number of staff per 1,000
connections, are found to be high when compared to other well-run water
utilities. This is particularly serious with the FWSC. An improvement of 10
percent to 15 percent for SWSC and YWSC, and of 15 percent to 20 percent for
FWSC by the year 2000 is considered reasonable. This could be done through a
human resource planning program which allows a gradual reduction of staff,
accompanied by job enrichment. The measures to achieve this would include:
(a) a recruitment rate which is lower than the retirement rate; (b)
redeployment of underutilized staff to areas where they can be more gainfully
employed; (c) an active in-house staff training program to upgrade staff



- 28 -
capThility and to retrain transferred staff; and (d) improved staff beniefit
incentives.  The proposed targets would be included in an action plan (para.
5.1') to be carried out by the water supply companies.
').15     Proposed target levels for improvements in metering coverage,
syst -m leakage and statting levels are shown in the schedule of performance
indicators in Annex 18.  During negotiations assurances were obtainied from the
Liaoning GoCveriuiiiemt thiat it would cause the water companies to carry out a
program for the improvemuenit of their metering coverage, system leakage anid
staffing levels, which would enable them to acheive the target performance
indicators on the specified dates as shown in the Annex.
Shenyang Urban Transport Organization
5.16      Responsibility for urban transport management in Sheniyang is vested
in three bureaus (Urban Construction Bureau, Transport Bureau and Public
Security Bureau) with separate responsibilities. A project office, the
Shenyvang Urban Special Projects Leadership Group Executive Office, has been
established as a permanient office with one of its objectives being to
coordinate project activities among the responsible bureaus. The Road
Maintenance Division (RMD), under the Urban Construction Bureau, is
re.ponsible for the maintenance of the 107 first and second class roads
,carrying about 70 percent of the total road traffic) in Shenyang. Total road
surface area for these two road categories in Shenyang accounts for roughly
5.6 percent of the city area. RMD employs about 1,100 consisting of 775
workers, 125 engineers and technicians, 100 equipment operators and 100
equipment maintenance staff, and is divided into eight maintenance teams. The
third and fourth class roads in the city are maintained by seven District
Construction Bureaus. Annex 20 provides details of road inventory and
conditions. The Traffic Brigade (under the municipal Public Security Bureau)
is responsible for traffic enforcement, road safety, installation and
operation of traffic signals, and for road markings and signs. The Bureau of
Communications is responsible for public transport, and is the authority that
regulates bus, trolley and taxi companies.
The Public Transport Companies
5.17      In Shenyang there are two public transport companies: one dealing
with bus transport, the Shenyang Bus Company, and the second, the Shenyang
Trolley-Bus Company, focussing primarily on trolley-bus service. The two
companies carry about two million passengers daily. Between the companies
there are about 680 buses and about 500 trollies operating over a total of 54
routes for a total route length of aobut 550 km. At end-1989 there were about
8,600 and 7,800 employees for the Bus Company and the Trolley-Bus Company
respectively (see operating statistics of the companies in Annex 21).
5.18      Not only do the companies provide public transport, but they also
assemble some of their vehicles and do all maintenance including major
overhauls. Major overhauls are also performed in their facilities for other
municipalities in the province, but there is not adequate capacity to keep
pace with the demand. Most of the facilities in the two organizations are
nearing 40 years old. There is therefore a large scope for improvement in
technical design, quality of construction and efficiency of maintenance
operations. Both organizations began a modernization program about three
years ago with the construction of new facilities. Capacities for major
overhauls are expected to increase by as much as 100 percent for the Bus
Company and about 33 percent for the Trolley-Bus Company with the furnishing



- 29 -
of new equipment from the project. Production targets of the public transport
colmipaniy workshops are shown in Annex 18, and will be reviewed annually witt
the companies.
Project Accounting
5.19      Project accounts will be maintained by LUCRPO in accordance with
the State Construction Unit Accounting System. This system sets out detailed
accounting definitions for capital works, cash control, receivables, payables,
and specific rules for capitalizing and expending of various items. It also
identifies a number of basic monthly, quarterly and annual reporting formats
including funds flow statements and balance sheets. The construction
accounting system is comprehensive and is adequate for the proposed project.
Audits
5.20      The Foreign Investment Audit Bureau of the State Audit
Administration (SAA), established in 1983, is responsible for audits of all
Bank/Association-financed projects. Under the proposed project, actual audit
of water company statements and of project accounts would be performed by the
respective municipal audit bureaus. The Liaoning Provincial Audit Bureau
(LPAB) would be responsible for auditing the consolidated accounts and
forwarding the reports to SAA for final review. This arrangement is
satisfactory. During negotiations, assurances were obtained from the Liaoning
Government that it would cause SWSC, FWSC and YWSC to furnish annual financial
statements, certified by SAM or an acceptable auditor, to the Association
within six months of the end of each financial year commencing 1992. In
addition, assurances were obtained that the following accounts would be
similarly audited and provided to the Association: (a) project accounts of
LUCRPO for the water and urban transport components; (b) the water companies'
total capital expenditures, including construction works financed by internal
sources; and (c) the Special Account.
5.21      Both SWSC and FWSC have recently developed their own internal audit
capability to conduct periodic examinations of the accounts of each operating
unit and subsidiary. YWSC engages the service of a single auditor whose
function is limited to spot checks. The object of these examinations is
primarily to test for arithmetical accuracy and compliance with accounting
regulations and contract terms. The first State Audit Administration audit
for the project (para. 5.20) scheduled for 1992 will include a review and
commentary on the adequacy of the internal controls and internal auldits of the
three water supply companies. During negotiations understanding on this was
reached and recorded in the minutes of negotiations.
Insurance
5.22      Until recent years, insurance coverage was not available from the
dom3stic insurance industry and all enterprises had to rely on self-insurance.
The water supply entities of the proposed project are currently sufficiently
liquid to self-insure all their assets adequately against normal levels of
risk. However, in view of the large investments now planned, assurances were
obtained during negotiations that the Liaoning Government would cause SWSC,
FWSC and YWSC to make adequate arrangements to have their assets insured to
the satisfaction of the Association.



- 30 -
VI. FINANCE
Financial System
6.1       The three water supply companies of Shenyang, Fuxin and Yingkou
(SWSC, FWSC, and YWSC) are all self-accounting entities under their respective
municipal governments. They must follow financial regulations issued by MOF
for all public enterprises, and procedures developed by MOC and local
financial authorities. Within that framework, the water companies enjoy broad
autonomy for managing their own financial affairs. With few exceptions, they
realize revenues which have in the past covered their operating requirements
and left them with reasonable liquidity. Most surpluses, however, have been
remitted to various levels of government; in turn, the government has provided
all the funds necessary for investment in approved capital projects on a grant
basis.
6.2       As with most state enterprises, the water companies are not
permitted to retain more than a nominal amount of their earnings (in various
Special Funds), and the cash corresponding to the earnings that are retained
must ultimately be applied to certain specified categories of expenditures or
investments. Thus, a water company's incentive to realize revenues in excess
of its obligations is limited. Also, familiar indicators of financial
performance, such as rate of return, have very limited applicability to
financial analysis of a Chinese water company.
6.3       Changes in progress in China's economic environment will inevitably
result in changes in the financial system. Already, with the introduction of
loan financing, consolidated accounts of the water companies should in the
future more accurately reflect the full value of construction work-in-
progress. In the future, some adjustment in the system of special funds
accounting will likely be necessary, as allocations to some of these funds are
directly related to historically valued gross fixed assets, while expenditures
from those funds are vulnerable to price changes.
6.4       While the financial system is adequate, the Government has begun
considering adjustments to accommodate changes in the economic environment.
To support the adjustment process, study tours have been included in the
training program under the proposed project, so that senior managers and
officials of relevant units may visit carefully selected utilities and
organizations abroad and thereby gain exposure to the approaches used
elsewhere in coping with inflating costs and extensive use of debt for
financing investments (see Annex 15).

1990 are in the Project File and summarized in Table 6.1. The balance sheets
have been adjusted to include construction work-in-progress, which is carried
on the books of construction units, but not consolidated into the water
companies' own operating accounts (para. 5.10).

6.6       Shenyang Water SuRply Company (SWSC).  During the period 1986 to
1990, sales volume increased by about 17 percent while operating revenue
increased by about 180 percent. The modest growth in operations is largely
attributed to water shortages. The water revenue increase was mainly due to
substantial tariff increases in 1987 and 1988. With these adjustments, SWSC
was able to maintain its operating ratio within about 70 percent. Energy,
salary and administration costs increased most prominently during the period,
reflecting the combined effects of price reforms and high inflation. The rate
of return peaked at 38 percent in 1988 as a result of tariff increases, and
declined in the following years.



- 32 -
6.7       Fuxin Water Supply Company (FWSC).  During tlhe 1986-1990 period.
S'ales inicreased by about 10 percent while operating revenue increased by .,bu,t
20() percent. Low population growth and a serious effort by the municipalit'.
to erntorce water conservat ion measures were the main reasonis for ttlit slow
growthl in sales.  The substantial increase in revenue was, again, thle res;ult
of major tariff increases implemented during 1987 and 1990. Although F'WSC's
financial performance improved significantly in 1987, it was not able to
maintain healthy financial ratios. During 1988 and 1989, the operating rat io,
were over 90 percent, and the rates of return dwindled to less than 4 percent..
This was mainly the result of unprecedented high inflation. From 1987 to 1>88
alone, production costs for the water supply company had risen by about 40
percent.
6.8       Yingkou Water Supply Company (YWSC).  From 1986 to 1990, sales
increased by about 20 percent while revenue increased by about 140 percent.
The water revenue increase came mainly from the industrial sector which
experienced two major rate increases over the period. YWSC also suffered from
relatively high operating costs and high inflation. During the period under
review, production costs rose by about 150 percent and the operating ratio
averaged at about 90 percent.
6.9       By virtue of the financing -^rangements described in paras. 6.1 to
6.4, very little financial debt appears in the water companies' balance
sheets. At the end of 1990, current ratios were 2.0, 1.8 and 2.4 for SWSC,
FWSC and YWSC respectively. Up till 1989, accounts receivable from water
sales were unusually low, amounting to about 7 to 14 days' gross revenue.
This was attainable because major consumers pay their bills through direct
debits and delay in payment was uncommon (paras. 5.4 and 5.6 to 5.8). The
situation had since deteriorated for all three water supply companies, with
accounts receivable up to around 25 days of gross revenue for 1990. These
levels are still considered to be reasonable. Accounts receivable from other
sources represent the water companies' sideline business income for providing
contractual services for minor construction and installation services.
Collection of non-operating income is subject to more delays.
6.10      Valuation of Assets.  Up to the present, only about 90 percent of
the new fixed asset investments, virtually all financed by government grants,
were transferred upon project completion from the books of the construction
units to the water companies' consolidated balance sheets. The residual had
been expended to cover mobilization and preliminary costs, the residual value
of construction equipment which remained with the construction units,
relocation costs, inventory losses, and project related training and medical
expenses. As a result of this practice, all the water companies' fixed assets
are understated.
6.11      The Chinese financial system has thus far not adopted the concept
of revaluation of fixed assets. Because of incipient changes in the price
structure and high inflation experienced by the water companies in the past
few years, revaluation of assets will become increasingly relevant in the
longer term. As this matter will have nationwide impact, it cannot be
addressed in the context of the proposed project. Rather, it will be
discussed as part of the continuing policy dialogue between the
Bank/Association, MOF and SAA.

6.14      SWSC.  During the 1991-1997 period, due to capacity limitations and
strict conservation measures, water sales are not expected to increase
significantly except for the year when the proposed Dahuofang scheme comes
onstream. Average annual growth in sales is expected to be about 5 percent.
Net fixed assets in operation are expected to increase by about 600 percent
over current levels. Average annual capital expenditure over the period is
expected to be about two times the 1988-1990 average level.
6.15      FWSC.  Over the forecast period, FWSC is projected to maintain high
rates of growth. Sales are expected to grow about 75 percent (8 percent
p.a.), net fixed assets in service about 420 percent, and average annual
capital expenditure about four times the average 1988-1990 level.
6.16      YWSC.  From 1990 to 1994 sales are expected to grow at an average
rate of 14 percent p.a.. No major construction is planned beyond 1994 when
the proposed project schemes are scheduled to be completed.
6.17      If the water companies are to maintain their financial viability in
the light of the projected system growth to meet increasing demands, the
forecasts imply that SWSC, FWSC and YWSC will need to increase their average
water tariffs by about 90 percent, 95 percent and 57 percent respectively by
1997. These represent compound annual growth rates of about 10 percent, 10
percent and 6 percent in current terms. Based on forecast rates of inflation,



- 35 -
in real terms, ttiere will be no increase for YWSC, while that for SWSC and
VWSC will be less thian 4 percent. The urban water supply tariff study to be
conducted under the project (para. 4.5 with draft terms of reference in Annex
3) would review the existing bases of water charges and design tariff
structures for the water supply companies that would allow them to meet their
financial objectives.
6.18      On the basis of these projected sales and revenues, the forecasts
show that SWSC, FWSC and YWSC would produce satisfactory financial results.
However, the water companies also face many uncertainties which might prevent
them from actually achieving these results. In order to enable them to direct
their attention to the long-term financial impact of their investment
decisions, the water supply companies need to adopt a systematic approach to
planning their financial affairs and extend their planning horizon. During
negotiations, assurances were obtained from the Government of Liaoning that it
would cause SWSC, FWSC and YWSC to furnish to the Association, by October 31
of each year commencing October 31, 1991 and ending October 31, 1996,
financial forecasts containing incomes statements, sources and applications of
funds, and balance sheets for the next eight years.
6.19      With the aid of such a plan, the water supply companies will be
better equipped to initiate and monitor the measures necessary to maintain
financial viability. Assurances were also obtained from the Government of
Liaoning that it would cause SWSC, FWSC and YWSC to take measures, including
but not limited to tariff adjustments, to ensure that their operating revenues
are sufficient to meet the aggregate of their total operating expenses and
financial obligations. For this purpose, the water companies' operating
revenues will need to cover: (a) costs directly chargeable to operations,
including provisions for depreciation and allocations to the maintenance
special fund; (b) interests and other charges on debt which are chargeable to
operations; (c) debt repayments in excess of the amounts repayable from funds
accumulated to provide for depreciation and maintenance; (d) required
allocations from net income for the special funds for employee benefits and
production development; (e) other required allocations from net income, if
any; and (f) increases in working capital.

6.21      Under the existing financial system, the water supply companies
have been able to use internally generated funds, accumulated under special
fund accounts earmarked for system development, to cover investments in
renovation, minor structural and network expansions and major repairs.
Construction work carried out under these funds comprise about 16 percent, 5
percent and 8 percent of the total investment programs of SWSC, FWSC and YWSC.
The proposed project components account for 55 percent, 39 percent and 90
percent of each company's investment program for the period. The proposed IDA
credit will meet 17 percent, 22 percent and 50 percent of the financing
requirements of SWSC, FWSC and YWSC.
6.22      The bulk of the capital investments of the water companies has
traditionally been financed by government-allocated funds. However, records
show that substantial amounts of collected funds, which are related to
operations of the water supply sector, have actually been remitted to
governmental authorities in the past. These include, inter alia, capacity
augmentation charges, self-supply water resource charges, above-quota penalty
charges and surplus earnings of the water supply companies. Thus, while
capital investment funding may appear to come from the government, a
substantial percentage of this should actually be accounted for as funds
generated from the sector itself. During the project construction period of
1990-95 alone, these sources are estimated to amount to 21 percent, 4 percent
and 15 percent of total sources of funds for SWSC, FWSC and YWSC respectively.
6.23      To ensure that all user charges collected by the :.ubsector,
including those not reflected in the revenue statements of the water supply
companies, would indeed be clearly earmarked for the expansion of the water
supply subsector, these funds were included as "sources of funds" for the
water companies in the financial projections. During negotiations assurances
were obtained from the Government of Liaoning that it would cause SWSC, FWSC
and YWSC to maintain a self-financing ratio of 25 percent. This ratio is
defined as cash generated by the water subsector that is available for
investment, i.e the sum of internally generated cash and all other user
charges collected by the water supply subsector less operational requirements,



- 37 -
expressed as a percentage of the annual average of the capital expenditures
for the current year, the previous year and the following year. Detailed
calculation of self-financing ratios for the three water supply companies over
the forecast period is included as Annex 24.
Public Transport Charges
6.24      For the last five out of six years, the Bus Company has been
operating at a loss. The Trolley-Bus Company has also been operating at a
loss for the last three years. Public transport fare increases are under the
control of the Municipal Government. Over the past forty years there have
been three fare ircreases: in the 1950's, in 1986 and in 1988. These fare
increases have not been able to generate sufficient revenues, however, for the
public transport companies to cover recurrent expenditures, and the companies
must rely on municipal government subsidies to make up shortages (see Annex 25
on income statements of Shenyang Bus and Trolley-Bus Companies). In turn,
sufficient funds have not generally been allocated for major overhauls of
buses and trolleys, replacement of obsolete equipment, and purchase of new
equipment to increase capacity. As part of the proposed project, technical
assistance will facilitate a study to review the operations and financial
situation of these two companies, and to make recommendations on fare
requirements and measures to improve their operations. These recommendations
will be reviewed with the Association, and plans for their implementation
would be formulated.
Municipal Finances
6.25      The municipalities of Shenyang, Fuxin and Yingkou are expected to
provide all remaining funds not met by other sources for the overall
investment program of their respective water supply companies. During the
project implementation period, the financial projections show that these
requirements would be on the order of Y386 million for Shenyang, Y260 million
for Fuxin and Y36 million for Yingkou.
6.26      Simplified historical and projected revenue statements of the three
municipalities for the period 1987-1994 are displayed in Annex 26. It is
estimated that overall urban sector spending, including capital outlays, would
make up about 10 percent of total expenditures in all three cities, of which
about 10 to 20 percent would be for water, including equity contributions for
the proposed project schemes. During negotiations, assurances were obtained
from the Liaoning Government that it would provide or cause the municipalities
of Shenyang, Fuxin and Yingkou to provide the necessary local currency funds
required for the execution of the project. Understanding on the Project
Implementation Schedule and funding schedule were reached during negotiations
and recorded in the minutes.
VII. ECONOMIC. SOCIAL AND ENVIRONMENTAL CONSIDERATIONS
Proiject Justification
7.1       The three cities are important industrial centers with gross
industrial outputs in 1989 estimated at Y24.9 billion (US$4.8 billion) for
Shenyang, Y1.6 billion (US$0.31 billion) for Fuxin, and Y4.0 billion (US$0.77
billion) for Yingkou. The project would allow water supply demands for
industrial and domestic needs to be fully met till the year 2000, and would
make significant improvements to urban transport in Shenvang, needed to



- 38 -
sustain economic growth and improve the standard of living of the population
in these cities.
Least-Cost Considerations
7.2       The selection of the water supply components for the three cities
has been subjected to rigorous least-cost analysis, which has been fully
documents in feasibility reports carried out by consultants (included in the
Project File). The process used included a comprehensive search of
alternative schemes which can be used to meet the pattern of growing water
demands of the three cities described in Chapter III. Potential schemes
considered are:
Shenyang.
(a)  Pipeline from Dahuofang Reservoir, with water purification.
(b)  Development of Phase 2 Shifeshi Wellfield in the Liao River basin,
with iron and manganese removal.
(c)  Development of Zhou Jai/Da Fan Wellfield in the Hun River basin,
with water purification.
Fuxin.
(a) Pipeline from Naodehai Reservoir with water purification.
(b) Development of Raoyang Aquifer with fluoride removal.
(c) Further exploitation of groundwater from the Daling River basin.
Yingkou.
(a)  Pipeline from Zhou Jia Reservoir for potable water supply, and
intake at Daliao River, pipeline and water purification plant for
industrial water supply.
(b)  Utilization of Erdaofang Reservoir water with pipeline and water
purification.
(c)  Development of Shang Tun Reservoir source at Biliu River.
The selected alternatives are those under item (a) for each of the three
cities, and are the least cost alternatives using discount rates of 5, 10 and
15 percent. The results have been confirmed by sensitivity tests with
deviations in capital and operating costs from plus to minus 15 percent. The
alternatives considered are not mutually exclusive. Rather they represent
potential sources which may all have to be eventually developed as demand
continues to grow. This explains the water resources development program
outlined in Annex 2.
Proiect BenefitL
7.3       The improved water supply would provide much needed drinking water
to the population, as well as inputs for industrial development in the three
cities. Benefits from the water components would come through improved health
conditions of the city residents, particularly those residing in lower income
areas, from: better water supply availability; improvement of water source
quality; and reduction of ingress of contaminated groundwater into the watcr
mains due to elimination of an intermittent water supply and the introduction
of adequate measures to control system leakage. The urban transport component
would relieve traffic congestion and improve road safety in central city areas
of Shenyang. Benefits would accrue from personal savings associated with
shorter travel times, savings in vehicle operating costs, reduction in



- 39 -
accident costs and reduction of air pollution. Additional project benefits
would also arise from thie introduction of better financial discipline, and
improvements in management and planning of urban services.
7.4       There are, however, difficulties in quantifying health benefits in
water supply, and in determining appropriate industrial benefits to be
attributed to water supply improvement. Projected water revenues using
average water tariffs have, therefore, been used as proxy benefits to compute
the internal rate of return (IRR) of the water supply components. These are
found to be about 4 percent for Shenyang, 8 percent for Fuxin and 5 percent
for Yingkou (Annex 27). In conducting the analysis, all costs and benefits
have been calculated in 1990 prices. Sensitivity tests assuming deviations in
costs and benefits from minus 10 percent to plus 10 percent have also been
carried out (Annex 27, page 4). The results show the IRR to range from 2.3 to
5.7 percent for the Shenyang component, 5.7 to 9.5 percent for the Fuxin
component, and 2.5 to 7.2 percent for the Yingkou component. The results are,
in fact, the minimum IRPs for the project components, since consumers have
dAmonstrated willingness to pay several times the average tariff for
adiitional water supply (para. 7.5).
7.5       The average incremental costs (A.I.C.F)12 of water supply for the
three cities, have also been computed.  These  .:e found to be from YO.39 to
YO.58 per ml for Shenyang, YO.63 to YO.89 per -' for Fuxin, and YO.57 to YO.78
per m3 for Yingkou at real discount rates fro 4 to 10 percent, and in 1990
constant prices. Annex 28 provides details oL the computations. Currently
industries pay from YO.60 to Y3.00 per m3 for water exceeding their quotas
(para. 6.12). While the charging method lacks consistency, these rates
actually exceed the A.I.C.s calculated. A study on water tariffs (para.3.7)
is proposed as part of the project to establish the method for determining the
tariff structure for the three water supply companies, in order that they
would reflect the A.I.C., as well as allowing the companies to meet their
financial obligations.
7.6       For the urban transport component, taking the package of corridor
improvements as a whole, the ERR was calculated to be 43 percent against a
base case of "do-nothing". Furthermore, the first year rate of return (FYRR)
was calculated to be about 47 percent, indicating that the improvements are
urgently needed. The basic assumptions used in the computation are shown in
Annex 29. This high economic rate of return is consistent with experience
elsewhere for low-cost traffic improvements involving improvements in traffic
control at intersections and segregation of incompatible traffic flows. A
sensitivity test with a 20 percent increase in cost and a 20 percent reduction
in the benefits reduced to the ERR to 29 percent, indicating that the
investments would still be worthwhile. Furthermore, eliminating the personal
travel time benefits to cyclists or public transport users would have only a
minor impact on the ERR. In fact about 91 percent of the benefits accrue to
motor vehicle users (excluding public transport vehicles), 88 percent of which
are from savings in vehicle operating cost (VOC) and 12 percent from personal
travel time. The transport component for road safety is considered as a
important adjunct to the corridor improvements inasmuch as it includes traffic
signals, signing, lane marking and traffic enforcement. No separate ERR was
calculated for this component.
12   The average incremental cost (A.I.C.) is an approach to obtain the
long run marginal cost.  When consumers are found to be willing to
pay the A.I.C. foe additional water, investments to expand the
system can be considered to be justified.



- 40 -
7.7       No attempt has been made to calculate the ERR of the road
maintenance, public transport and technical assistance/training components.
For road maintenance, additional road resurfacing equipment will enable the
Department to substantially increase the capacity for road resurfacing; for
public transport, additional equipment will enable both companies to reduce
the time in the shop as discussed in Annex 18, and technical
assistance/training will strengthen operations in road maintenance and public
transport, and traffic enforcement. Since improvements in traffic flows and
in the public transport companies are dependent upon improved operation,
training in these areas have been included in the training program (Annex 15).
AffordabilitY
7.8       The current domestic water rates represent approximately 1.3 to 2.5
percent of the family income of those residents on the lower quartile of the
three municipalities, assuming the prevailing usage levels. Incomes used for
these calculations represent only cash income and exclude subsidies in kind.
It is therefore clear that the tariff levels are affordable and allow some
margin for future increases.
7.9       Currently, industries are paying between 0.5 and 5.0 percent of
their production costs for water includ-ig the penalty rates mentioned in
para. 6.12, despite the high water usage. The figures are low by
international standards. As price controls are gradually tightened, it is
expected that industries would take measures to conserve water. Eventually
some of the water price increases will be passed on to the consumers.
Environmental Impact
7.10      Environmental impact assessment reports in accordance with the
requirements of the Government of People's Republic of China have been
prepared by the project municipalities and are found satisfactory by the
Association. The three water supply components involve the extraction of
water from existing reservoirs. The environmental impact of reduced flow in
the rivers due to water extraction has been studied. For Fuxin the Naodehai
Reservoir was constructed essentially for silt control. The conversion of the
reservoir for water supply will require some changes in the reservoir
discharge pattern. Studies on the operation of the reservoir have been
conducted to optimize the use of the water for water supply and, at the same
time, continue its function of silt control. The Zhou Jia Reservoir for
Yingkou was designed for irrigation, but is no longer used for agriculture
because the farmers downstream have found other sources to meet their needs.
The Dahuofang Reservoir for the Shenyang supply is a multiple purpose water
source. With a greater allocation of the water for urban water supply, some
diminishing of the agricultural water supply will result. The decrease in
agricultural water supply is minimal, however, given the comparatively smaller
size of urban water needs. Nevertheless arrangements have been made to
compensate agricultural users, and to augment the diminished supply with
improvements in groundwater supply irrigation systems.
7.11      The project would make useful contributions toward improving the
environment, which would include, inter alia, reducing the reliance on
groundwater thus lessening ground subsistence problems, strengthening
environmental protection institutions, increasing fuel efficiency through
decreasing traffic congestion, and reducing traffic accidents through road
safety improvements. The project has also included a component to address
problems associated with water pollution control, and has provided for
technical assistance to prepare a second phase project, which is part of a



- 41 -
comprehensive plan for water resource management and water quality improvement
itn Liaoriintg.  The second project will focus on irban wastewater management and
water pollution control. Draft terms of reference for project preparation are
in Annex 14.
Project Risks
7.12      Significant pricing policy reforms and various institutional
changes are required in the project, and there are possible risks that the
Liaoning Government and the three Municipal Governments may not succeed in
pushing these reforms through fully. Substantial local counterpart funds are
required and because of the heavy competition for development funds, there is
a possibility of a shortfall of funds required for the project. The project is
part of a longer term development program, and project benefits are linked to
the successful implementation of other phases of the program. There is a risk
that the Liaoning Government may not be able to raise the necessary resources
to fully implement the program, thus diminishing the potential benefits of the
project. To minimize these risks the project has provided for technical
assistance to study tariffs and tariff structures which can be afforded by the
users, and at the same time achieve the required economic objectives.
Agreement has also been reached with the Liaoning Government to provide or
cause the municipalities to provide in a timely manner the necessary
counterpart funds required for the project. The proposed project would also
provide support to build up an effective planning institution which would
follow up on the recommendations of the development program, review the
proposals periodically, prepare implementation plans and seek the investment
resources required.
VIII. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
8.1       Assurances were obtained from the Government of Liaoning on the
following:
(a)  A study, with the assistance of experienced consultants and based
on terms of reference and timing satisfactory to the Association,
would be carried out on the pricing of water resources for
agriculture ani urban water supply in the Hun-Taizi River Basin
region, as well as on other measures for water conservation; and .o
discuss the results of the study with the Association, and
implement its recommendations after they have been agreed upon
(para. 3.7).
(b)  Consultants satisfactory to the Association would be engaged to
advise on detailed design, preparation of contract documents,
supervision of project construction, conducting of project-related
studies, and providing managerial, technical and financial advice
to the water supply and public transport companies (para. 4.14).
(c)  The project municipalities would maintain the project offices
established to facilitate implementation, with functions and
responsibilities satisfactory to the Association, and with
competent staff in adequate numbers (para. 4.19).
(d)  Resettlement of persons affected by the project would be carried
out in a manner and according to a schedule which is satisfactory
to the Association (para. 4.31).



- 42 -
(e) The training program would be implemented in accordance with the
agreed plan and timing satisfactory to the Association (para.
4.16).
(f) SWSC, FWSC and YWSC would carry out an action plan for the
improvement of their metering, system leakage and staffing levels
to the satisfaction of the Association (para. 5.15).
(g) SWSC, FWSC and YWSC would furnish audited financial statements to
the Association within six months of the end of each financial
year. The following accounts would be similarly audited and
provided to the Association: (i) project accounts of LUCRPO for
both the water and urban transport components; (ii) the water
companies' total capital expenditures, including construction works
financed by internal sources; and (iii) the Special Account (para.
5.20).
(h) SWSC, FWSC and YWSC would make adequate arrangements to have their
assets insured to the satisfaction of the Association (para. 5.22).
(i)  SWSC, FWSC and YWSC would furnish to the Association, by October 31
of each year commencing October 31, 1991, financial forecasts
containing incomes statemn_r.ts, sources and applications of funds,
and balance sheets for the next eight years (para. 6.18).
(j) SWSC, FWSC and YWSC would take measures, including but not limited
to tariff adjustments, to ensure that their operating revenues are
sufficient to meet the aggregate of their total operating expenses
and financial obligations (para. 6.19).
(k) SWSC, FWSC and YWSC would maintain a self-financing ratio of 25
percent (para. 6.23).
(1) It would provide or cause the municipalities of Shenyang, Fuxin and
Yingkou to provide the necessary local currency funds required for
the execution of the project (para. 6.26).
8.2       Understandings were also reached on the following, and recorded in
the minutes of the negotiations:
(a) The various steps to be taken and the schedule for resettlement
(para. 4.30).
(b) The first State Audit Administration audit for the project
scheduled for 1992 will include a review and commentary on the
adequacy of the internal controls and internal audits of the three
water supply companies (para. 5.21).
(c) The Project Implementation Schedule and the funding schedule (para.
6.26).
8.3       With the above assurances and understandings, the proposed project
would be suitable for an IDA credit of SDR 54.2 million (US$77.8 million
equivalent) to the People's Republic of China at standard IDA terms with 35
years maturity including 10 years grace.

S. Four hours of supply in the morning, and four hours in the evening.
6.  City water mains are fully pressurized for two hours in the morning and two hi%rs in the evening.
7.  Residences in upper floors often receive very little or no water supply. Industries fare better as they are normally
equipped with large water storage tanks which are filled up during the pressurized periods.

- 55 -                             Annex 0
Page 1 of 2
C(HINA
LIAONING URBAN INFRASTRUCTURE PROJECT
Project Components
1.   Shenyang Watcr Suppty. Construction of a transmission pipeline (400,000 m3 per day
capacity) of about 51 km. in length comprising a single 2200 mm dia. or twin 1600 mm dia.
from Da Huo Fang Reservoir to treatment plant site, and about 32 km. of 500 mm to
1200 mm dia. distribution pipelines. (Intake works at Da Huo Fang Reservoir, and the
treatment plant for Da Huo Fang water would be constructed using Shenyang's own resources.
Parts of these works have already been started.)
2.   Fuxin Water Supply: Construction of intake works, sedimentation basin and pumping station
at Nodehai Reservoir, about 74 km. of 900 mm to 1600 mm dia. transmission pipeline (100,000
m3 per day capacity) from Nodehai sedimentation basin to treatment plant site, 2 boostcr
pump stations, water treatment plant, and about 14 km. of 900 mm to 1200 mm dia. supply
line to the city network.
3.   Yingkou Water SuDPlV: Construction of about 17 km. of 500 mm dia. trn'Ismission pipeline
from Zhou Jia Reservoir to the existing San Dao Ling pipeline; a water i L:a; . Daliao River
(120,000 m3 per day capacity) for industrial water supply; a pumping station, about 40 km. of
600 mm to 900 mm dia. transmission pipeline, and about 49 km. of 300 mm to 900 mm dia.
distribution pipelines for the Daliao River industrial water supply; and improvements to
existing water treatment plants.
4.   Water Pollution Control: procurement of equipment and instruments for environmental
pollution monitoring, and a mobile laboratory and equipment for water quality monitoring
work.
5.   Shenyang Urban Trans2ort:
a. Improvement works for about 44 km. of arterial road corridors through limited pavement
widening, geometric changes, signalization at intersections, separation of motorized and non-
motorized traffic flows and the purchase of road safety equipment;
b. procurement of maintenance and repair equipment for the bus and trolley-bus major depots
and garages; and improving operation management for bus routing and scheduling,
maintenance and financial control; and
c. procurement of road maintenance and road safety (line marking and signing) equipment and
the implementation of a computerized road maintenance system.
6.   Project Support Technical Assistance (Engineering-related):
a. Advising on project management and construction supervision for both the water and
transport components.
b. Preparation of a second phase project which will focus on water pollution abatement.
7.   CaDacitv Building Technical Assistance (Institution-related):
a. Providing to the 3 water supply companies technical experts to advise on management of
water supply operations, including enforcement of water regulations, improving quality of



- 56 -                                Annex 6
Page 2 of 2
system records, detection of leakage, assessment and reduction of unaccounted-for water,
improvement of meter repair and waterworks maintenance; and of financial experts to advise
on the keeping of an appropriate public utility form of accounting system, financial planning
for the companies, and pricing requirements designed in conjunction with "b." above.
b. Providing technical experts to public transport companies in Shenyang to advise and train
the staff on measures to improve public transport operations through better route planning,
vehicle maintenance, and rationalizing public transport fares.
c. Providing technical experts for strengthening traffic regulations and their enforcement,
introducing effective measures to reduce accidents, and initiating public education program
with the Public Security Bureau.
d. Training to staff of the project offices, the Bureau of Urban Construction, and the 3 water
supply companies (see details in Annex 15).
8.    Policy Development Technical Assistance (Institution-related):
a. Conducting a study on pricing and other measures to achieve efficient use of water
resources in the Hun/Taizi River basins, and to study appropriate policies and rate structurcs
for water supply in the cities of Shenyang, Fuxin and Yingkou.
b. Studying effective methods for industrial pollution reduction through process modification,
recycling and other measures that can also achieve production efficiency; source treatment in
difficult industrial sectors, and strengthening of the environmental protection laws and
regulations.

CHINA
LIAONING URBAN INFRASTRUCTURE PROJECT
Resettlement Plan
1.    The liaoning Urban Construction and Renewal Project Office (LUCRPO) and the various project offices of
Shenyang, Fuxn and Yingkou have prepared the preliminary design for the project, which includes land acquisition and
resettlement. This has been approved by the Construction Commissions of Liaoning and the three municipalities. Full
detailed surveys of the exact areas required by each contract and a head count of the population to be resettled have
been conducted. The detailed surveys have been submitted to the Urban Planning Bureaus of the three municipal
governments for approval. The population in the affected areas has been frozen to avoid any further in-migration.
2.    For Shenyang and Yingkou all the land to be permanently acquired has been secured. Land for the headworks,
pumping stations and treatment plant, and the resettlement of a small number of persons in Fuxin are under
negotiations, and no difficulties are envisaged. The remaining and bulk of the land required by the project would be
for the construction of the transmission pipelines, and would involve the use of agricultural land. It is planned to acquire
the land for these on a temporary basis to be returned to the farmers after the pipes are laid and buried underground.
While the actual disruption to farming activities on the used land would be for about one growing season, it is envisaged
cash compensation of up to three years of the value of crops would have to be paid to allow for loss of soil fertility from
disturbance of the topsoil for pipelaying. In addition special consideration will be given to the stripping and storage of
topsoil during construction to allow it to be restored later. In many cases the farmers have required that they be
engaged to do this work themselves in order to ensure preservation of quality of the topsoil.
3.    About 18 months prior to the actual need to use the land, the project offices will prepare a detailed plan for
resettlement of the population with information provided by relevant municipality bureaus on land available for housing
construction and housing units that can be supplied from housing enterprises. The time required for this process will
be about I month. The housing plan would estimate the number of housing units required. If all the necessary housing
units can be provided by housing enterprises, they would be purchased. The time for arranging and delivery of the
housing units will then be about 6 months, depending on the amount of housing units in stock. If all or some of the
necessary housing units are not available, they should be designed and constructed under the project. The time required
for this process is dependent upon the work scale, and estimated to be slightly longer than purchasing (about 9 months).
Therefore this would be on the critical path for the resettlement plan.
4.    With the housing and resettlement plan determined, the project offices will contact local district officials to discuss
implementation details of the plan. The time required for this would be about 0.5-1 month. The actual work of
arranging relocation of the residents would be done by the local district officials. They will negotiate with residents and
decide when the relocation would take place. This process may run over a period of 8-9 months, before residents in an
area are completely moved. The residents would be paid moving expenses (the amount may diminish, if moving is not
prompt) to encourage moving. When all the residents have moved out, the area will be demolished, a process which
takes about one month. The total time required for resettling occupants in an area is thus estimated to be 12-15
months.
5.    Detailed land acquisition and resettlement schedules are contained in the the project implementation schedule
in Annex 13, and is also shown below.
1991        1992         1993
Shenyang:   land leased               1,200 mu     1,200 mu     1,200 mu
persons resettled          300

persons resettled          65

- 78 -                                          Annex 1S
Page 3 of 4
that the manager might gain an appreciation of new    Timing
developments in urban transport planning, design,
mnanagement and operations.                            18.       The training program is to be completed by
December 31, 1994.
15.  'Ihe Project will emphasis training with the intent to
reduce the dependence on foreign advisors for the design
of the traffic improvement schemes and in the operations
of the public transport system. Foreign advisors will be
used to supplement the training program with practical,
on-the-joh-training (OJT) Courses planned to be given
in (China are: (i) T'raffic engineering and road corridor
deign practices; (ii) Traffic regulations, enforcement and
management;and (iii) Public transport operations and
workshop management. In fact formal training was
started in traffic engineering for engineers in the Project
Office and the Design Institute as part of the corridor
design activity for the Froject. This training will continue
through the corridor implementation stage and into the
monitoring and evaluation phases.
Training for Project Staff
16.  Project staff training includes intensive courses for
project management personnel. Area of project staff
training would cover contract law which covers contract
programming and administration, arbitration, and other
related subjects, construction supervision and quantity
survey.  The project will contract the services of
consultants specializing in this field to prepare and
conduct the training in Shenyang. In order to achieve
maximum benefit, this course would have to be
conducted at an early stage of the project.
Organization for executing Training Program
17. A training unit consisting of 5 persons will be set up
within the Liaoning Urban Construction and Renewal
Project Office to be rcsponsible for coordinating the
training efforts under this project. The unit will be
responsible for working out the details of the training
program, arranging training courses and field trip,
arranging overseas training, selecting trainees, inviting
lecturers, and preparing the lists of training equipment
and materials to be procured.
Cost Estimates
17.      The total cost of training and management
development over the project period (1990 to 1994) is
estimated to be about Y5.29 million (US$1.13 million) of
which Y3.89 million (US$0.83 million) are foreign costs
and Y1.4 million (US$0.3 million) are local costs. The
amount is shared equally between water supply and
urban transport personnel. The amount includes about
YI.24 million (US$0.26 million) for the procurement of
training and laboratory equipment and development of
training materials. Details of the cost estimate are found
in page 4 of this annex.

Operation Performance Monttors
Water SuDDlV Comnanies
For the purpose of measuring perfonnance, a number monitors to gauge operating efficiencies have been suggested to
be reported by managers of the water supply companies Because of variations in conditions between systems, it is not
possible to establish standard performance targets, and notional targets will be set for each water supply company with
the monitoms used principally for the purpose of gauging whether improvement in performance has been achieved. The
performance monitors to be kept by the water companies are to be as follows:
Shenvan       Fuxin        Yinizkou
1. Domestic supply metering (%)
1990                        66            49            72
1995                        87            78            93
2000                        100           100           100
2. Unacoounted for water (%)
1990                        17            16            16
1995                        12            12            12
2000                        10            10            10
3. Staff manning level
(in staff per 1,000 conns.)
1990                       5.50          8.69          5.77
1995 (proposed)            5.13          7.82          5.38
2000 (proposed)             4.88          7.25          5.11
In addition, in order togauge improvement or deterioration in operating performance, the watersupply companieswould
also record and report on a yearly basis (a) the average cost of fuel per unit of production; (b) the average cost of
chemicals per unit of production; (c) the frequency distribution of recorded daily lowest water pressure maintainable in
the system; and (d) the percentage of water samples that have failed to pass quality tests.

L.IAONING URBAN INFRASTRUCTURE PROJECT
Assumptions to Financial Proiections
1.    Projections of main financial statements (Income Statements, Balance Sheets and Flow of Funds Statements) of the
three water companies of Shenyang, Fuxin and Yingkou for the period 1991-1997 are in the Project File. Financial forecast
models were developed during project preparation using Lotus-123 on personal computers. Detailed projections of
investment program, loan parameters, fixed asset formation, operating costs, water demand and special fund calculations are
available in the project file. 1'hc following summarizes the bases on which projections are made.
2.    Inflation and Exchange Rates. Domestic inflation rates used in the projections are 10% pa. for 1991, 8% p;... for
19'2 and 5% p.a. thereafter. Foreign price increases are estimated at 9.0% p.a. for 1991. 1.1% p.a. for 1992 and 2.95% p.a.
thereafter. Fxchange rate for January 1991 was Y5.22 per US Dollar. It is assumed that exchange rate adjustments will,
on average, maintain purchasing power parity over the projection period.
INCOME STATFMENT
3.    Water Sales. Projections of sales are based on the "minimum demand" and maximum capacity figures given in Annex
2. Unaccounted-for water is estimated at 15% of production. It is assumed that whenever demand exceeds supply, the
shortfall would be borne by all categories of consumer proponionally.
4.    Average Tariffs. Average prices over the projection period for SWSC, FWSC AND YW.SC are derived as follows:

5.    Operating Costs. Energy, material, salary and water resource costs are based on detailed projections of unit costs
and production levels resulting from the expansion programs. Combined depreciation and maintenancc rate for
pipeworks is about 7%. For other fixed assets, depreciation is taken at 4.0% to 4.5% and maintenance at 3.3% to 4.5%
of gross fixed assets in operation. Sales tax is at 3% of gross operating revenue. Activity of the Depreciation Special
Fund, Maintenance Special Fund and Staff Bonus Special Fund are derived in the tables entitled "Special Fund
Calculationse available in the Project File.
6.    Interest & Amortization Excenses. Detailed terms and calculations of each loan are included in the Project File.
The IDA Credit is assumed to be onlent at 5.5% p.a. interest and 0.5% p.a. commitment charge, 15 years maturity
including 5 years of grace. In compliance with current practice, interests during construction are not capitalized, they
are charged to operations as due. After construction is completed, interest and amortization for each loan are being
paid for out of the depreciation fund of the scheme it finances, up to 80% for the first three years and up to 50%
thereafter. Any remaining repayment will be met as a distribution of net income. Detailed calculations are included
in the table "Interest and Loan Repayment Met by Depreciation Special Fund" in the Project File.



- 96  -                                      Anttiex  23
Page 3 of 4
7.     Distribution ot Net lncomc. Staff Benefit and Rewined Earming reprcsent annual alkloations to these tAo
internal speciail funds. They arc expecied tO increase in accordance with inflation. Remittance to (iosernnient represents
net income after authoriied distributions. Ihese funds are being remitted in cash to various lcvcs of authorits, usually
on a monthly basis.
BAIANCE SIElT
8.     Inventories have been projected to take into account general inflation as well as expanded letvls of operatlon
and construction.
9.     Accounts Receivable. Accounts receivable from water sales are assumed to be 2q to 47, or about 7 to 14 days'
revenue. 'Ihis is generally attainable because major consumers pay their bills through direct debit from bank accounts
and delay in payments is uncommon. Accounts receivable from other sources represent the water companics' sideline
business income for providing contractual services for minor constructions and installations. Collection of non-operating
income is usually more difficult and often delayed.
10.    Fixed Assets. The Chinese financial system has so far not adopted the concept of revaluation of fixed assets.
In fact, in the past, only a portion (about 90% in the water supply sector) of the total cost of construction was
transferred upon completion from the books of the construction units to the consolidated balance sheets of the operating
water supply companies. In the projections, construction work-in-progress are consolidated in the water companies
balance sheets, and are assumed to be 100% accounted for upon completion. Detailed schedules titled "Capital
Expenditures", "Fixed Asset Additions" and "Construction WIP" are included in the Project File.
11.    Government Funds includes all paid in equity, plus transfers to Government for loan repayment mct by
distribution of net income.
12.    Special Funds is derived in the "Special Fund Calculations" tables. It represents the cumulative balance
remaining in the various special funds at the end of a given year.
FLOW OF FUNDS STATEMENT
13.    Capacity Augmentation Charges represent collections from industrial users for quota increases. Current charges
are Y200, Y300 and Y600 per m3 per day for consumers of Shenyang, Fuxin and Yingkou respcctively. Per unit charge
is expected to increase in line with inflation for SWSC and FWSC. Quantity is a function of incremental sales to
industries.
14.    Self-supply Water Resources Fund represents payments made by industrial consumers for the use of self-supplied
water. Current rates are Y 0.15, Y 0.03 (to be increased to Y 0.10 from 1991 on) and Y 0.03 per m3 per day for uscrs
of Shenyang, Fuxin and Yingkou. These rates are not expected to change with inflation for Shenyang and Fuxin over
the forecast period. Quantity is detailed in the demand forecast schedules.
15.    Above-quota Penalty Charges are applied to the difference between actual consumption and quota of major
industrial consumers. This system differs from an increasing block tariff as the revenue is not treated as part of the
water revenue. The water companies have the discretion to charge up to S times the regular rate. An average of 3
times ts used for projection purposes.
16.    Government Equity Contributions have been taken to cover all local cost requirements for capital constructions
not provided by other sources. This is generally consistent with projections made by municipal finance bureaus. Dctails
are included in the "Capital Expenditures" tables in the Project File.
17.    Special Fund Constructions represent capitalized renovations, minor expansions, overhauls and staff housing
financed by the various special funds. Operation of special funds are derived in the detailed "Special Fund Calculations"
tables.
18.    Increase in Workin, Cat,ital represents the excess of the current year's current assets (net of cash) over current
liabilities, less the excess of the previous year's current assets (net of cash) over current liabilities.



- 97 -                                        Annex 23
Page 4 4f 4
19.    Net Special Fund Fxenditurcs. Gross special fund expenditure is the aggregate amount actually spent from
the various special funds in a particular year. Net expenditure is derived by subtracting capitalized special fund
consiructions and debt service met by depreciation and maintenance funds from the gross figure.

U rla.i Irans%p4rt C'nolntptent
l coiiiiitiic Anat4 sis: IBasic Assumptionis atd I)ata
I            1uBenclt\ lor the tr.attic corridor irnprocnicnts werc ciiculdtcd using savings in schicle operating cosis.
perso)nallinmc xis ing~ nd re.lucedl a.ciluent . ,t ( h.ingc in thxe pairameters with projcCt iinpr ccnicilts Acre dcrivcd
trom the transport modclling undcrt.ikcn during the projcct prcparation. T1he total network VO('s were estitr(jed using
a cV iiputer model 'Ahich calculates (he cos.ts based upon the each corridor's volume/capacity ratio. Based upon this
ratio, the model estimates the vehicle spcds and time from which the VOC arc calculated and summcd tO give the
nrtAocrk operating costs. Ihe differcnce in the network opcranng costs from the "do-nothing" case with the improved
case, as dectrmined hy the model, are the henefits.
2.            T1he capital costs of the components are hased on the required improvcments as determined during the
fcasibhility studies. Costs included land, works and equipment for each project component (all taxes and duties were
excluded). Annual costs for increased road maintenance has also been included.
3.           The base case comprises the present transport network plus any committed projects. Sincc there are
network effects and the complcmcntary nature of these corridor improvements, it is inappropriate to compare each
corridor improvements with the base case. TIhe analysis included estimates for the future strcams of economic costs and
benefits comrlred with the base case and allows these to be compared on the basis of the economic rate of return
(E.RR). All financial costs were converted to reflect the opportunity cost of the resources within the China economy.
4.            Since the vehicle operating cost (VOC) savings are primarily time related and since there were no
reduction in corridor lengths, the following values for vehicle times were used:
Value of TIime Saved (Yuan/Veh-Hr)
Bic_Cle               Car          Truck          Bus          Trolley
V(C                         0.00           4.70          3.83          10.27         11.17
Time Savings /             0.25           1.38          0.00          22.00         20.00
Ij     I be user time costs assumes a value of Yuan 1.0/hr while on
" paid" or worker time and Yuan 0.25/br while on unpaid time.
The savings are based upon running times taking into account the effect of road design and condition on the basic
operating costs of fuel, oil, tires, vehicle maintenance and depreciation. The average number of passengers was estimated
by vehicle type.
5.            The traffic management corridor improvements show an overall ERR of 43% which is well in excess of
the cost of capital in China. The high ERR is consistent with experience elsewhere in low-cost traffic management
improvements. The first year rate of return (FYRR), an indicator of the urgency of the project, is also a very high 47%.
A sensitivity analysis indicates that the combined effect of a 20% increase in project costs and a 20% reduction in
benefits reduces the ERR to 29%. It is also worthy to note that personal travel time benefits contributed only about
17% to the total benefits; when these benefits were deleted from the benefit stream, the ERR was reduced to 36%.
Benefits from reduction of a xident costs account for about one and one-half percent of the total benefits. The
contribution to the ERR ot'ie accident reduction benefit is about one to two percent. Thus the net ERR using only
vehicle operating costs would be about 41%. The economic evaluation summary is given in Table I of this Annex.
